<h2><a id="post-143320-_lf97qkussdwx"></a>New KimJongRAT PE Variant</h2>
<p>This section details the new KimJongRAT variant that uses PE files as final payloads.</p>
<p>The initial file of the execution chain is an LNK file, but we do not yet know how attackers distribute these files. Figure 1 shows the execution flow of the most recent KimJongRAT variant.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143332" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143332" style="width: 600px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143332 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-597346-143320-1.png" alt="Diagram depicting a multistage cyber attack involving various malware components and processes like dropper, downloader, decoy, DLLs, and orchestrator, interacting with Command and Control servers." width="600" height="751" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-597346-143320-1.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-597346-143320-1-352x440.png 352w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-597346-143320-1-560x700.png 560w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-597346-143320-1-768x961.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 600px) 100vw, 600px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143332" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 1. Malware execution chain of the latest KimJongRAT PE variant (<a href="https://www.flaticon.com/packs/files-131" target="_blank" rel="noopener">icon</a> <a href="https://www.flaticon.com/packs/business-online-2" target="_blank" rel="noopener">sources</a>).</figcaption></figure>
<ul>
<li><strong>Step 1: </strong>When double-clicked, the initial LNK file downloads an HTML Application (HTA) file from an attacker-controlled CDN account, saves it to disk and runs it as shown in Figure 1</li>
<li><strong>Step 2: </strong>The HTA file drops three embedded files <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sexoffender.pdf</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">user.txt</span> to disk
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Sexoffender.pdf</span> is a decoy PDF file opened by the victim's default PDF reader</li>
<li>The HTA file executes the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span> loader</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><strong>Step 3: </strong>The loader uses two payload URL strings in the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">user.txt</span> file to retrieve two more files named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span>
<ul>
<li>These LOG files are a new KimJongRAT stealer component and an orchestrator</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><strong>Step 4: </strong>The orchestrator sends the collected information and data to a command and control (C2) server and awaits commands from the attackers</li>
</ul>
<p>To more fully understand these steps, let’s examine the associated files.</p>
<h3><a id="post-143320-_p2jpmtfrgy78"></a><strong>PE Variant Initial LNK File</strong></h3>
<p>When double-clicking <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/3b0a3bd5b790e5f130e7819550613b7e0194a3475f553285a1b7dc18ecca9d02" target="_blank" rel="noopener">one of the initial LNK files</a>, the file uses the Windows tool <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span> to change the current directory to the Windows <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> folder (shown in the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Local base path</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Command line</span> arguments in Figure 2) . It then uses the Windows tool <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">curl.exe</span> to download an HTA file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pdf.hta</span> from a legitimate CDN provider at <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cdn.glitch[.]global</span> into the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> directory. The attacker abuses this service to host the next and subsequent stages of the malware.</p>
<p>The URL for the HTA file contains a parameter <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">v</span> with the string <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1740535190239</span>. This string is an <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epoch_(computing)" target="_blank" rel="noopener">epoch date</a> that translates to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Wednesday, February 26, 2025, 1:59 a.m.</span> (GMT).</p>
<p>Finally, the LNK runs the downloaded HTA file using the Windows tool <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">mshta.exe</span> as shown in Figure 2.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143343" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143343" style="width: 852px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143343 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-601742-143320-2.png" alt="Command prompt screen displaying file paths and system details, with highlighted sections around the local base path and command line arguments. " width="852" height="275" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-601742-143320-2.png 852w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-601742-143320-2-786x254.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-601742-143320-2-768x248.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 852px) 100vw, 852px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143343" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 2. Execution related LNK information as shown in <a href="https://github.com/Matmaus/LnkParse3" target="_blank" rel="noopener">LnkParse3</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/3b0a3bd5b790e5f130e7819550613b7e0194a3475f553285a1b7dc18ecca9d02" target="_blank" rel="noopener">This LNK file</a> contains unique metadata that can be used to find additional samples. Figure 3 shows the drive serial number, Windows OS version and machine ID of the system where the LNK file was created. Additionally, there is a Korean language string <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">응용 프로그램</span> (translated: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">application program</span>) in the extra data section.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143354" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143354" style="width: 772px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143354 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-604525-143320-3.png" alt="Screenshot of system information and specifications, including drive types, volume names, and other serialized property details. Three sections are highlighted in blue boxes. " width="772" height="408" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-604525-143320-3.png 772w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-604525-143320-3-768x406.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 772px) 100vw, 772px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143354" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 3. Metadata from the LNK file as shown in <a href="https://github.com/Matmaus/LnkParse3" target="_blank" rel="noopener">LnkParse3</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><a id="post-143320-_7cifgbgqs34w"></a><strong>PE Variant First Stage HTA File</strong></h3>
<p>The LNK sample we analyzed downloaded and saved an HTA file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pdf.hta</span> to the Windows <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> directory. <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/9c9136fc8a279ce395997dd42c075e265c6daec14b13bbe4237a4178769d270e" target="_blank" rel="noopener">This HTA file</a> contains obfuscated VBS code. Additionally, the HTA file has three embedded payloads appended after the code as Base64 text.</p>
<p>Figure 4 shows an excerpt of the HTA file with the obfuscated VBS code and the start of the Base64-encoded payloads.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143365" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143365" style="width: 535px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143365 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-607416-143320-4.png" alt="A screenshot of a computer screen displaying a script or programming code in an integrated development environment or text editor. The displayed code includes numerical data, strings, and various programming functions." width="535" height="406" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143365" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 4. Excerpt of the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pdf.hta</span> file content as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Figure 5 shows the deobfuscated version of this HTA file with the truncated Base64-encoded payloads.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143376" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143376" style="width: 1704px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143376 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-610383-143320-5.png" alt="Screenshot of a computer code script displayed in a text editor. Indicated by arrows from top to bottom: Start of Base64 string for second payload. Start of Base64 string for first payload. Start of Base64 string for third payload. " width="1704" height="668" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-610383-143320-5.png 1704w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-610383-143320-5-786x308.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-610383-143320-5-768x301.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-610383-143320-5-1536x602.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 1704px) 100vw, 1704px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143376" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 5. Deobfuscated version of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pdf.hta</span> as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Base64 string for the first payload starting with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">JVBERi0xL</span> is decoded through the Windows tool <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">certutil.exe</span> and dropped as the decoy PDF file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sexoffender.pdf</span> into the Windows <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> directory. It is then opened by the default application for PDF files.</p>
<p>The Base64 string starting with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">aHR0cHM6L</span> for the second payload is decoded and dropped as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">user.txt</span> to the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%</span> folder.</p>
<p>The third Base64 string starting with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">TVqQAAMAAA</span> is decoded and dropped as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span>, also to the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%</span> folder. This HTA file then runs <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span> using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">rundll32.exe</span> using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span>'s only exported function named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">s</span>.</p>
<p>The dropped <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">user.txt</span> is a text file containing URLs to the same CDN sub-directory that hosts the malicious HTA file, as shown in Figure 6.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143387" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143387" style="width: 602px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143387 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-614672-143320-6.png" alt="Screen capture showing a Notepad window with two URLs listed, both pointing to LOG files. " width="602" height="100" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143387" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 6. The content of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">user.txt</span> as shown in Windows Notepad.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/f4d9547269e0cd7a0df97e394f688e0eb00b31965abd5e6ad67d373a7dc58f3b" target="_blank" rel="noopener">The last dropped file</a> is named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span>, and it downloads the files from the URLs in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">user.txt</span> and executes them.</p>
<h3><a id="post-143320-_fv7h2abg57f0"></a><strong>Second Stage Loader sys.dll</strong></h3>
<p>The second stage loader named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span> is a 64-bit DLL internally named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">baby.dll</span>. It has a single exported function named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">s</span> that contains all the malware's functionality.</p>
<p>When this function is called with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">rundll32.exe</span>, it first checks whether the malware is running on a virtual machine or sandbox as shown in Figure 7. If that is the case, the loader deletes itself and quits. If not, it creates a mutex named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">co_sys_co</span> and starts a sub-thread.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143398" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143398" style="width: 754px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143398 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-617482-143320-7.png" alt="A screenshot of a computer code editor displaying several lines of C++ programming code, involving functions for file handling and system registry access." width="754" height="325" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143398" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 7. Decompiled source code of exported function <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">s</span> from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span> as shown in <a href="https://hex-rays.com/ida-pro" target="_blank" rel="noopener">IDA Pro</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The sub-thread checks if any previously dropped payloads are present in the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%\net</span> directory. It uses this directory to store downloaded payloads from the attacker’s CDN stager URL.</p>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span> loader expects any files downloaded to this folder to be encrypted data binaries with the first 16 bytes being the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RC4" target="_blank" rel="noopener">RC4</a> decryption key for the remaining bytes. When it finds a file in this folder, it decrypts, executes and finally deletes the file.</p>
<p>After creating the sub-thread, the malware reads the URLs from the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%\user.txt</span> file previously dropped by the HTA file. It appends the date and time in epoch format as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">?v=<em>[epoch time]</em></span> to each URL string. Afterwards, it contacts the CDN service to download the RC4-encrypted file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span> into the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%\net</span> folder to load it reflectively.</p>
<p>This <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span> file is the new KimJongRAT stealer component. It endlessly runs a loop that only exits if the file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%\micro.log.zip</span> is present. This file is created by <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span> and contains the victim’s stolen information and data.</p>
<p>When <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">micro.log.zip</span> is detected, the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sys.dll</span> loader downloads the second RC4-encrypted file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> from the CDN server and stores it as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">notepad.log</span>. As soon as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">notepad.log</span> is written to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%\net</span>, the sub-thread reads, decrypts, executes and deletes it. This decrypted file is the main orchestrator that implements network, backdoor and information-stealing functionality.</p>
<h3><a id="post-143320-_nfs0e6d37jpt"></a><strong>Third Stage Orchestrator and Backdoor</strong></h3>
<p>The downloaded payload <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> is internally named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">NetworkService.dll</span> and has a compilation timestamp of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">December 3, 2024, 7:36 a.m.</span> UTC. Figure 8 shows its <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/visualstudio/debugger/debug-interface-access/querying-the-dot-pdb-file" target="_blank" rel="noopener">PDB file path</a>.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143409" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143409" style="width: 604px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143409 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-621056-143320-8.png" alt="Screenshot displaying a debug window focused on raw data properties, including a highlighted 'PDB FileName' field showing a path to a file." width="604" height="210" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143409" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 8. PDB file path of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span> as shown in <a href="https://www.mitec.cz/exe.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener">EXE Explorer</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>As noted in Figure 8, the software has a PDB file path that includes the string <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\research\Spyware\Advanced\Covaware</span>. A 2019 article by ESTsecurity describes a campaign named <a href="https://blog-alyac-co-kr.translate.goog/2223?_x_tr_sl=auto&amp;_x_tr_tl=en&amp;_x_tr_hl=en&amp;_x_tr_pto=wapp" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Operation Giant Baby</a> where attackers used malware with the same name in activity relating to our <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/new-babyshark-malware-targets-u-s-national-security-think-tanks/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">BabyShark article</a> from the same year.</p>
<p>This <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> file is the main orchestrator that handles output created by the other downloaded file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span>. While <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> is primarily responsible for the network communication and backdoor functionality, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span> is responsible for stealing credentials from browser and email or FTP clients.</p>
<p>The main orchestrator has a single exported function named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">fool</span>, which contains the majority of the malware’s functionality. The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">DllMain</span> entry point is only used for various initialization routines. These routines create multiple directories associated with the base C2 URL and file paths that the malware uses later.</p>
<p>As a unique victim ID, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> uses the volume serial number. If the volume serial number cannot be obtained, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> uses a combination of the computer and username for the victim ID. It encodes this alternative ID value as a Base64 string, as shown in Figure 9.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143420" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143420" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143420 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-623990-143320-9.png" alt="Screenshot of computer code in an editor, highlighting functions and variables related to URL processing and unique ID generation. Sections of the code are annotated with comments. From top to bottom are: C2 URL. Unique ID. Alternative unique ID. " width="900" height="405" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-623990-143320-9.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-623990-143320-9-786x354.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-623990-143320-9-768x346.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 900px) 100vw, 900px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143420" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 9. Decompiled C2 base URL creation function from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> as shown in <a href="https://hex-rays.com/ida-pro" target="_blank" rel="noopener">IDA Pro</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>However, this alternative ID is not used throughout the malware’s code and thus seems to be leftover code from earlier versions of this malware. After establishing the unique ID, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> calls the exported function <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">fool</span> before finally writing the clipboard data into a file.</p>
<p>The exported function <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">fool</span> shown in Figure 10 starts four threads before infinitely looping through a sleep call.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143431" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143431" style="width: 683px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143431 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-627062-143320-10.png" alt="Screenshot of a computer code in an IDE, featuring functions related to thread management and keyboard logging. The top line has &quot;fool&quot; highlighted in yellow." width="683" height="285" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143431" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 10. Decompiled C2 string creation function from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> as shown in <a href="https://hex-rays.com/ida-pro" target="_blank" rel="noopener">IDA Pro</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>These threads are named as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main_thread</span></li>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">clipboard_log_to_netkey_file</span></li>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">keylogger_log_window_title_and_keys</span></li>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">keylogger_flush_to_netkey_file</span></li>
</ul>
<p>The first thread named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main_thread</span> shown below in Figure 11 implements the network, backdoor and information stealing functionality. The other three threads are dedicated to recording keystrokes, window titles and clipboard information.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143442" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143442" style="width: 593px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143442 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-630031-143320-11.png" alt="A screenshot of a computer code in an integrated development environment, featuring a function named &quot;main_thread&quot; highlighted in yellow that involves various operations such as loading modules, setting internet options, uploading files, and implementing a sleep command." width="593" height="374" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143442" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 11. Decompiled <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main_thread</span> from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main64.log</span> as shown in <a href="https://hex-rays.com/ida-pro" target="_blank" rel="noopener">IDA Pro</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The network communication is implemented in an infinite loop that uploads collected data and requests commands from the C2 server. This malware implements three methods to communicate with the C2 server. To upload data or files, it uses the <a href="https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Reference/Methods/POST" target="_blank" rel="noopener">HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">POST</span></a> method with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">multipart/form-data</span>, which we will subsequently describe as HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">POST multi</span>, or <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">application/x-www-form-urlencoded</span>, which we will call HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">POST app</span>. To download data, the malware uses an <a href="https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Reference/Methods/GET" target="_blank" rel="noopener">HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GET</span></a> request.</p>
<p>Figure 12 shows the initial network capture where the stolen browser data and the system information are sent to the C2 server.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143453" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143453" style="width: 871px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143453 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-633720-143320-12.png" alt="Wireshark screenshot displaying HTTP headers and other network request details with portions of the text redacted. Some of the information is also truncated. " width="871" height="633" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-633720-143320-12.png 871w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-633720-143320-12-605x440.png 605w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-633720-143320-12-768x558.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 871px) 100vw, 871px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143453" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 12. Initial network communication with the C2 server as shown in <a href="https://www.wireshark.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Wireshark</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>At first, the file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">micro.log.zip</span> from the<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;"> %localappdata%</span> directory is copied into the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> directory as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">micro.log.zip_</span>. This file is then uploaded to the C2 server with an HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">POST multi</span> request and the hard-coded boundary string <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">----------sdfaffi3457839sfhjkaskl</span>. Before it is uploaded as a value of the key <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">file0</span>, the ZIP archive is XORed with the key <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0xFE</span>.</p>
<p>Additionally, two keys <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">val</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">id</span> with the values <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">delete</span> and the volume serial number are sent to the C2 server. The former is most likely a note that the original file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">micro.log.zip</span> is deleted after its copy gets uploaded, while the latter is used to associate the ZIP archive to a specific victim.</p>
<p>The HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">POST multi</span> method is always used to send file data, as is the same schema described above:</p>
<ul>
<li>Key: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">val</span>, value: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">delete</span></li>
<li>Key: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">id</span>, value: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;UniqueVictimID&gt;</span></li>
<li>Key: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">file0</span>, value: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;XORedFileData&gt; (XOR key is always 0xFE)</span></li>
</ul>
<p>The HTTP POST app method is either used to send encrypted data or to send the server-side delete command (further described as HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">POST app delete</span>). This <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">delete</span> command is used on the server side to clear out the appropriate command or feature queue. The schema is as follows for data:</p>
<ul>
<li>Key: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">id</span>, value: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;UniqueVictimID&gt;</span></li>
<li>Key: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">nm</span>, value: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;FeatureName&gt;</span></li>
<li>Key: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">val</span>, value: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;XORedFileData&gt;</span> (XOR key is always <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0xFE</span>) or <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">delete</span></li>
</ul>
<p>Next, the malware sends an HTTP GET request to the C2 URL ending with the victim's unique directory, which it creates from the volume serial number and the filename <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">history.log_</span>. If the file is not already on the C2 server, the malware performs the following activities:</p>
<ul>
<li>Collecting various system information</li>
<li>Writing it into a file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">history.log</span> in the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%appdata%</span> directory</li>
<li>Creating a copy of it in the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> directory named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">history.log</span></li>
<li>Sending it to the C2 server using the HTTP <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">POST multi</span> method</li>
</ul>
<p>It collects the following system information in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">history.log</span>:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hostname</li>
<li>IP address</li>
<li>Computer name</li>
<li>Windows user account name</li>
<li>Disk drive information (available drives, volume names, file system names, drive types)</li>
<li>Operating system (version and product name)</li>
<li>System type (32-bit or 64-bit)</li>
<li>Internet Explorer version</li>
<li>Start menu items</li>
<li>CPU information</li>
</ul>
<p>The initial communication sends the victim's data to the C2 server, and any additional actions from the C2 server are based on that initial data. Table 1 shows other information that is periodically uploaded to the C2 server.</p>

<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Table 2. List of backdoor commands.</span></p>
<h3><a id="post-143320-_7ablo2hj9pw4"></a><strong>Third Stage KimJongRAT Stealer</strong></h3>
<p>The other downloaded file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">net64.log</span> is the main KimJongRAT stealer component. The decrypted file is internally named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dwm.dll</span> and has a compilation timestamp of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">December 15, 2024, 4:03 a.m.</span> UTC. It has three exported functions <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">init_engine</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">main_engine</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">stop_engine</span>. Only the first function contains all the functionality, while the latter two only redirect execution to the entry point <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">DllMain</span>, which is empty.</p>
<p>When <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">init_engine</span> is executed, the malware first resolves a list of API functions using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetProcAddress()</span>. All function strings are encoded by a simple substitution cipher where characters are changed to others according to a mapping table. The following Python script contains the reconstructed algorithm and can be used for decoding these strings:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-68537fda23932883114968" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-nums-button" title="Toggle Line Numbers"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-wrap-button" title="Toggle Line Wrap"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea  class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
import argparse

class KimJongRATTool:

CHAR_MAPPING = {

'!': '-', '#': ')', '$': ';', '%': '+', '&amp;': '=', '(': ':', ')': '#',

'*': '_', '+': '%', ',': '/', '-': '!', '.': '?', '/': ',', ':': '(',

';': '$', '&lt;': ']', '=': '&amp;', '&gt;': '^', '?': '.', '@': '}', '[': '{',

']': '&lt;', '^': '&gt;', '_': '*', 'a': 'm', 'b': 'q', 'c': 'f', 'd': 'h',

'e': 'x', 'f': 'c', 'g': 'l', 'h': 'd', 'i': 'p', 'j': 's', 'k': 't',

'l': 'g', 'm': 'a', 'n': 'z', 'o': 'r', 'p': 'i', 'q': 'b', 'r': 'o',

's': 'j', 't': 'k', 'u': 'y', 'v': 'w', 'w': 'v', 'x': 'e', 'y': 'u',

'z': 'n', '{': '[', '}': '@'

}

@staticmethod

def map_string(encoded_string: str) -&gt; str:

return ''.join(KimJongRATTool.CHAR_MAPPING.get(c.lower(), c).upper() if

c.isupper() else KimJongRATTool.CHAR_MAPPING.get(c, c) for c in encoded_string)

def decode_string(self, encoded_string: str) -&gt; None:

print(f'Decoded string: {self.map_string(encoded_string)}')

def decode_strings(self, file_path: str) -&gt; None:

with open(file_path) as f:

print('Decoded strings:')

for line in f:

print(self.map_string(line.strip()))

def main():

parser = argparse.ArgumentParser()

group = parser.add_mutually_exclusive_group(required=True)

group.add_argument('-f', '--file_path', type=str, help='(Absolute) File path with encoded strings.')

group.add_argument('-s', '--encoded_string', type=str, help='Encoded string.')

args = parser.parse_args()

kjrt = KimJongRATTool()

if args.file_path:

kjrt.decode_strings(args.file_path)

else:

kjrt.decode_string(args.encoded_string)

if __name__ == '__main__':

main()</textarea></div>

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0010 seconds] -->
<p>The same cipher is used to encode other sensitive strings related to the stealer's functionality.</p>
<p>Based on the list of decoded function strings, the stealer attempts to retrieve information from various popular browsers and FTP or email clients. Other sensitive strings related to the stealer functionality, like the browser extension ID, are encrypted by a simple XOR-based cipher.</p>
<p>The malware stores the stolen data in plain text and SQLite files in a directory <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%\<em>[RandomName]</em>.tmp</span>. An overview of the victim information is stored in the file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%\<em>[RandomName]</em>\micro.log</span>. This file contains the following information:</p>
<ul>
<li>Operating system information</li>
<li>CPU information</li>
<li>Process information</li>
<li>Start menu programs</li>
<li>Website/cookie/password information of supported browsers</li>
<li>Configuration and password information of supported email clients</li>
<li>Password information of supported FTP clients</li>
</ul>
<p>The malware also searches all supported browsers for multiple cryptocurrency wallet extensions shown in Table 3.</p>

<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Table 3. Searched for browser extensions with their corresponding IDs.</span></p>
<p>The extension IDs for each browser are stored in the file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%\<em>[RandomName]</em>\ext.log</span>.</p>
<p>Additionally, the malware steals various SQLite database files for supported browsers found in each browser’s user data directory. For example, for Google Chrome, these files can be found in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\<em>[UserName]</em>\AppData\Local\Google\Chrome\User Data\Default</span> for the default user. These database files contain detailed information about the user from browser features including bookmarks, history, saved passwords and installed extensions. The malware searches for the following in the database files:</p>
<ul>
<li>Cookies</li>
<li>Login data</li>
<li>Web data</li>
</ul>
<p>These files are copied to the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%\<em>[RandomName]</em>.tmp</span> directory and renamed by prepending the profile user and a browser indicator. The last file created in this directory contains the master encryption key derived from a browser’s <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Local State</span> file. This key is needed to decrypt sensitive browser data, such as stored passwords or cookies.</p>
<p>Finally, these files are compressed using the PowerShell <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Compress-Archive</span> command to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%\micro.log.zip</span>. This file is then uploaded to the C2 server by the orchestrator.</p>
<h2><a id="post-143320-_1ma0sxrgwh6h"></a>Previous KimJongRAT PE Variants</h2>
<p>We have also discovered other variants of this malware execution chain, dating back to at least August 2024. The first variants deployed 32-bit DLL files as the final stealer and orchestrator payloads, which is different from the latest variant that uses 64-bit DLL files. Also, the execution chain sometimes differs in the way that the second-stage loader drops the decoy PDF, or whether it uses the decoy PDF at all.</p>
<p>Other differences are that the initial LNK file does not use <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span> and curl.exe but instead <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">powershell.exe</span> with the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Invoke-WebRequest</span> command to download the next stage HTA dropper.</p>
<h2><a id="post-143320-_vjsygtub7b8w"></a>New KimJongRAT PowerShell Variant</h2>
<p>This section discusses the latest variant of KimJongRAT, which uses a PowerShell information and crypto-wallet stealer as its final payload. It is very similar to the PE variant in its functionality but focuses on only stealing system and browser data.</p>
<p>This execution chain uses a variety of file types and is carried out in multiple stages. The initial file is an LNK file as seen in Figure 13, which illustrates the full execution chain.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143464" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143464" style="width: 700px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143464 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-636695-143320-13.png" alt="Flowchart detailing a multistage malware attack involving several components like Downloader, Dropper, Decoy, Runner, Stealer, and Keylogger, each linked by directional arrows indicating the sequence of actions." width="700" height="1028" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-636695-143320-13.png 899w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-636695-143320-13-300x440.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-636695-143320-13-477x700.png 477w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-636695-143320-13-768x1128.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143464" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 13. Malware execution chain of the latest PowerShell variant (<a href="https://www.flaticon.com/packs/files-131" target="_blank" rel="noopener">icon</a> <a href="https://www.flaticon.com/packs/business-online-2" target="_blank" rel="noopener">sources</a>).</figcaption></figure>
<ul>
<li><strong>Step 1:</strong> When double-clicked, the LNK file downloads an HTA file from an attacker-controlled CDN account to disk and runs it, as shown above in Figure 13</li>
<li><strong>Step 2: </strong>When executed, this HTA file drops an embedded decoy PDF and a ZIP archive to disk</li>
<li><strong>Step 3: </strong>The decoy file is opened by the default installed PDF reader, and then files from the ZIP archive are extracted and saved to disk</li>
<li><strong>Step 4: </strong>From those extracted files, a PowerShell file loads the stealer and keylogger and sets the runner VBS script for persistence</li>
<li><strong>Step 5: </strong>The stealer sends the collected information and data to the C2 server and awaits commands from the attackers</li>
</ul>
<h3><a id="post-143320-_mv9iic16ew5e"></a><strong>PowerShell Variant Initial LNK File</strong></h3>
<p><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/a66c25b1f0dea6e06a4c9f8c5f6ebba0f6c21bd3b9cc326a56702db30418f189" target="_blank" rel="noopener">An example of an initial LNK file</a> (SHA256 hash: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">a66c25b1f0dea6e06a4c9f8c5f6ebba0f6c21bd3b9cc326a56702db30418f189</span>) submitted to VirusTotal is named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">성범죄자 신상정보 고지.pdf.lnk</span> (translated from Korean: “<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Sex Offender Personal Information Notification</span>”). This sample is almost identical to the sample we reviewed in the PE malware chain. The only difference is that it downloads a different HTA file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sfmw.hta</span> and uses a different value for the parameter <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">v</span> as shown in Figure 14.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143475" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143475" style="width: 851px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143475 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-640269-143320-14.png" alt="Image showing a Windows command prompt with text displaying file path and system information for a program. Some of the information is highlighted in red boxes. " width="851" height="247" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-640269-143320-14.png 851w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-640269-143320-14-786x228.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-640269-143320-14-768x223.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 851px) 100vw, 851px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143475" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 14. Execution related LNK data as shown in <a href="https://github.com/Matmaus/LnkParse3" target="_blank" rel="noopener">LnkParse3</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The LNK file’s metadata is identical to the one described in the latest PE malware execution chain.</p>
<h3><a id="post-143320-_3dl0gzyn401h"></a><strong>First Stage HTA File</strong></h3>
<p>The <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/02783530bbd8416ebc82ab1eb5bbe81d5d87731d24c6ff6a8e12139a5fe33cee" target="_blank" rel="noopener">downloaded <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sfmw.hta</span> file</a> is dropped into the Windows <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> directory. This file contains VBScript code, obfuscated with the same algorithm as the one in the PE variant. Unlike the PE variant, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sfmw.hta</span> only has two embedded payloads.</p>
<p>Figure 15 shows an excerpt of this HTA file with the obfuscated code and one of the two Base64-encoded payloads.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143486" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143486" style="width: 717px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143486 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-643245-143320-15.png" alt="Screenshot of a computer script written in VBScript, displayed in a text editor with numbered lines and syntax highlighting." width="717" height="355" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143486" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 15. Excerpt of the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sfmw.hta</span> file content as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Figure 16 shows the deobfuscated version of the HTA file with the truncated Base64-encoded payloads.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143497" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143497" style="width: 851px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143497 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-646826-143320-16.png" alt="A screenshot of a computer script written in VBScript displayed in a text editor with various commands for file manipulation and execution." width="851" height="270" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-646826-143320-16.png 851w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-646826-143320-16-786x249.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-646826-143320-16-768x244.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 851px) 100vw, 851px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143497" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 16. Deobfuscated version of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sfmw.hta</span> as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Figure 16 shows that the script within the HTA file uses <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">findstr.exe</span> with the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">/b</span> parameter to locate each Base64-encoded payload within the file text. Then, the script uses <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">certutil.exe</span> to decode the Base64 strings.</p>
<p>At first, the embedded payload starting with the Base64-encoded data <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">JVBERi0xLj</span> is dropped as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sexoffender.pdf</span> (same filename as in the PE variant) into the Windows <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> directory. This <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/455cea72b7cd2e2b6fc7bb09c946db03ea624f26fe32910e05a46a63d63e142c" target="_blank" rel="noopener">decoy PDF file</a> is then opened by the default installed PDF reader and seems to be a Korean form related to sex offenders, as shown in Figure 17.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143508" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143508" style="width: 603px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143508 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-649748-143320-17.png" alt="Image of a formal document in Korean, featuring a structured layout with headings, bullet points, and multiple sections of text. " width="603" height="571" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-649748-143320-17.png 603w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-649748-143320-17-465x440.png 465w" sizes="(max-width: 603px) 100vw, 603px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143508" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 17. PDF decoy document <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">sexoffender.pdf</span> as shown in <a href="https://www.adobe.com/acrobat/pdf-reader.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Adobe PDF Reader</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The second payload from the HTA file is a Base64-encoded string starting with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">UEsDBBQAAA</span>. This string is decoded and dropped as <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/50a392f1aa8b88d0818c2d8716d195e999bf439564ec6a895dcde4a0463ece13" target="_blank" rel="noopener">a ZIP archive</a> named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pipe.zip</span> to the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%localappdata%</span> folder. The files from this archive are extracted, and the PowerShell file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.ps1</span> is run. The other unpacked file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.log</span> is passed as an argument to the PowerShell file.</p>
<p>Figure 18 shows that the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pipe.zip</span> archive contains four files.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143519" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143519" style="width: 759px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143519 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-652766-143320-18.png" alt="A screenshot displaying a file explorer window with a list of four files, along with details including file size, packed size, and timestamps for modified, created, and accessed dates. All files have an attribute set to 'A'." width="759" height="99" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143519" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 18. Files contained in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pipe.zip</span> as shown in <a href="https://www.7-zip.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">7-Zip</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Components of this malware were created in September 2024, as shown in the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Modified</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Created</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Accessed</span> dates of the files <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.ps1</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.vbs</span>. The files <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.log</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">2.log</span> that contain the Base64-encoded PowerShell stealer were updated in March 2025.</p>

<h3><a id="post-143320-_l6twv74o2nvy"></a><strong>Second Stage PowerShell Stealer</strong></h3>
<p>The PowerShell file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.ps1</span> shown in Figure 18 is a simple loader that decodes and runs the Base64-encoded file <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;"><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/b1f9b450b97320de54f2450ace151b4f16444dc871f5e89487d52d862ce13cc2" target="_blank" rel="noopener">1.log</a></span> that is passed as an argument. It executes the PowerShell code with the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Invoke-Expression</span> alias <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">iex</span> as shown in Figure 19.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143530" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143530" style="width: 653px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143530 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-656337-143320-19.png" alt="Image of a code snippet in PowerShell using functions to convert a string from Base64 encoding." width="653" height="99" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143530" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 19. PowerShell code of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.ps1</span> as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The decoded script in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1.log</span> is a PowerShell stealer with backdoor functionality. This malware can be logically divided into three parts:</p>
<ul>
<li>Header</li>
<li>Malware functionality</li>
<li>Main function logic</li>
</ul>
<p>The header defines several variables and performs a simple anti-VM check as shown in Figure 20.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143541" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143541" style="width: 447px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143541 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-659165-143320-20.png" alt="Screenshot displaying a PowerShell script snippet with conditional logic to check for VMware and delete specific log files from a computer system." width="447" height="222" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143541" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 20. Variable definitions and anti-VM check of the PowerShell stealer as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The header part creates a new directory in the Windows <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%temp%</span> folder named after the system’s UUID retrieved from the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/cimwin32prov/win32-computersystemproduct" target="_blank" rel="noopener">WMI <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ComputerSystemProduct</span> class</a>, and it defines a few path variables and the C2 URL. Additionally, this part checks whether the victim host is a VMware virtual machine based on the UUID serial number value. If it is a VMware system, the malware deletes itself and then exits. However, this anti-VM check is flawed, as the retrieved UUID does not contain any VM-related strings in comparison to other fields of the same WMI class.</p>
<p>The second part of the malware is its functionality. This part consists of multiple functions, shown in Figure 21.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143552" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143552" style="width: 296px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143552 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-662082-143320-21.png" alt="Screen of code with syntax highlighting showing functions named UploadFile, Unprotect-Data, GetExWFile and several more, with line numbers." width="296" height="384" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143552" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 21. Folded functions of the PowerShell stealer as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>

<tr style="height: 48px;">
<td style="height: 48px; width: 24.3355%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Get-ShortcutTargetPath</span></td>
<td style="height: 48px; width: 75%;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">Retrieves the target path of a specified Windows shortcut by creating a COM object of </span><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WScript.Shell</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"> and using its </span><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CreateShortcut</span><span style="font-weight: 400;"> method.</span></td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 24px;">
<td style="height: 24px; width: 24.3355%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">RecentFiles</span></td>
<td style="height: 24px; width: 75%;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">Retrieves the target paths of all recent files (shortcuts) in the user's Windows account and appends them to a text file </span><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">recent.txt</span><span style="font-weight: 400;">.</span></td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 24px;">
<td style="height: 24px; width: 24.3355%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Work</span></td>
<td style="height: 24px; width: 75%;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">Described in more detail below.</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Table 5. Overview of the PowerShell functions used in the stealer.</span></p>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetBrowserData</span> function is designed to extract various types of data from multiple browsers, including <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/edge/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Edge</a>, <a href="https://www.google.com/chrome/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Chrome</a>, <a href="https://whale.naver.com/en/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Naver Whale</a> and <a href="https://www.mozilla.org/en-US/firefox/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Firefox</a>. This function uses another function named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetExWFile</span> to manage specific data associated with cryptocurrency wallet browser extensions. Figure 22 shows an excerpt of the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetBrowserData</span> function. This excerpt indicates the malware is still in development with many lines of code commented out.</p>
<figure id="attachment_143563" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-143563" style="width: 892px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-143563 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-665250-143320-22.png" alt="A screenshot of computer code with syntax highlighting, showing the function &quot;GetBrowserData&quot; with various coding elements." width="892" height="528" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-665250-143320-22.png 892w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-665250-143320-22-743x440.png 743w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/06/word-image-665250-143320-22-768x455.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 892px) 100vw, 892px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-143563" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 22. <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetBrowserData</span> function as shown in <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Visual Studio Code</a>.</figcaption></figure>

<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Table 6. Searched for browser extensions with their corresponding IDs.</span></p>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetExWFile</span> function retrieves files associated with these extensions, based on the specific handling procedures defined for each of the hash tables. The function begins by attempting to retrieve the encrypted master key from the local user's data for each browser.</p>
<p>If the browser process is running, it halts the process to avoid file access conflicts. Then, it navigates through all user profiles for each browser within the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">User Data</span> directory. For every user profile, it duplicates various data types, such as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Login Data</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Bookmarks</span>, to a new location.</p>
<p>For Edge, Chrome and Naver Whale, the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetExWFile</span> function processes data related to browser extensions. It receives the browser's name, the profile path and the profile name as arguments. After it duplicates the necessary data, the function enumerates all extensions installed for the user profile and appends this list to a text file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">extensions.txt</span>. If the browser process was initially running, this function restarts the process once it has copied all the data.</p>
<p>For Firefox, the function specifically copies certain files (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">key4.db</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">key3.db</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cookies.sqlite</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">logins.json</span>) associated with each user profile.</p>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CreateFileList</span> function scans all file system drives on the system, specifically targeting the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Users</span> directory on the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\</span> drive. It searches for files with extensions shown in Table 7.</p>
